Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/medium/am0c23069_0009.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/large/am0c23069_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c23069&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are the appropriate alternatives to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) for the large-scale energy storage applications because of the abundant resources and wide distribution of sodium on earth. O3-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> is a promising cathode material for SIBs due to its stable structure and low-cost raw materials. In this paper, we design and synthesize a powder consisting of submicrometer-sized O3-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> spheres (s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>) self-assembled with nanoflakes, which exhibits faster ion migration ability and strong structure robustness. The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique test reveals the higher apparent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> when compared with a normal NaCrO<sub>2</sub> powder with an irregular particle morphology<sub>.</sub> The s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> shows impressive electrochemical properties with a capacity of 90 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 50 C. In addition, outstanding cycling stability is shown when tested at 20 C, where a capacity of 90 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is maintained with a retention of 87% after 1500 cycles. Also, s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> is advantageous at high (50 °C) and low (−10 °C) temperatures. The full cells assembled employing Sb/C as the anode exhibit good rate capability with 85 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> obtained at 50 C.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D12203%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DShuo%2BWang%252C%2BFei%2BChen%252C%2BXiao-dong%2BHe%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D10%26contentID%3Dacsami.0c23069%26title%3DSelf-Template%2BSynthesis%2Bof%2BNaCrO2%2BSubmicrospheres%2Bfor%2BStable%2BSodium%2BStorage%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D12210%26publicationDate%3DMarch%2B2021" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291952&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrochemical cells"> Electrochemical cells </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292229&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Morphology"> Morphology </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292439&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Physical and chemical processes"> Physical and chemical processes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium"> Sodium </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292275&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transmission electron microscopy"> Transmission electron microscopy </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Chromium+Oxide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20chromium%20oxide">sodium chromium oxide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Submicrospheres&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_submicrospheres">submicrospheres</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Long+Cycle+Life&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_long%20cycle%20life">long cycle life</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20battery">sodium-ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Full+Cell&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_full%20cell">full cell</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">With the rapid increase in renewable energy converted from solar energy and wind energy, a low-cost energy storage system is urgently needed. Although lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been widely applied to portable electronic equipment and electric vehicles, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> the limited lithium resources trigger the concerns about the cost of large-scale energy storage grids. Hence, the low-cost alternatives to LIBs such as sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 3 to 6">(3−6)</a> and potassium-ion batteries (PIBs) <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 7 to 9">(7−9)</a> have been explored in recent years. Due to the abundant sodium resources, SIBs have been considered as the most appropriate candidate with a similar electrochemical reaction mechanism to LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 10 and 11">(10,11)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The cathode is the essential component of a battery, and varieties of SIB cathode materials have been studied. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 12 to 17">(12−17)</a> The layered transition-metal oxides have been studied widely due to their two-dimensional fast ion diffusion channel and high capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 18 to 21">(18−21)</a> As a typical layered SIB cathode material, O3-type NaCrO<sub>2</sub> shows good structure stability and a reversible capacity of about 120 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> based on the Cr<sup>3+</sup>/Cr<sup>4+</sup> redox. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> Nevertheless, NaCrO<sub>2</sub> synthesized through a solid-state reaction method usually shows poor rate and cycling performances due to its large primary particle size and irregular morphology. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22,23)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Many modification strategies to overcome the weaknesses of O3-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> have been reported so far, including carbon coating <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref24" aria-label="reference 22 and 24">(22,24)</a> and hetero-ion doping. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 25 to 27">(25−27)</a> However, little attention has been paid to the effect of morphology. On the other hand, cathode materials with regular spherical morphology have been proved to have enhanced rate and cycling performances. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 28 to 32">(28−32)</a> Lou et al. have synthesized P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> microspheres through a two-step self-template strategy. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> The Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> microspheres with a hierarchical structure enable good rate capability and capacity retention for sodium storage. Huang et al. have reported a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F material in spherical particles, which shows high rate performance and long cycle life for SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> These results reveal that a spherical morphology with a hierarchical structure can greatly improve the sodium storage performance of cathode materials. Moreover, the electrode material with spherical particles is easier to obtain uniform laminates, which is of great significance for practical application.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, we report a facile self-template method to fabricate O3-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> submicrospheres. First, Cr(OH)<sub>3</sub> submicrospheres are synthesized via a reported hydrothermal method. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Then, Cr(OH)<sub>3</sub> submicrospheres react with sodium acetate at 900 °C to obtain O3-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>. In this process, the spherical morphology can be maintained during the formation of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> despite the high calcination temperature. The O3-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> submicrospheres exhibit outstanding sodium storage ability with good rate capability and superior cycle stability.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Material Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p">A facile self-template strategy was employed to prepare NaCrO<sub>2</sub> submicrospheres, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>. First, a Cr(OH)<sub>3</sub> powder was prepared through a facile hydrothermal method. In brief, 2 mmol of acrylamide and 1 mmol of K<sub>2</sub>Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> were added into 70 mL of distillated water and stirred to form a homogeneous solution. Then, the solution was put into a 100 mL Teflon-lined autoclave and heated at 180 °C for 12 h. After being washed by distillated water and ethanol, the light green Cr(OH)<sub>3</sub> powder was obtained after drying at 80 °C.</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/medium/am0c23069_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/large/am0c23069_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Synthesis Process of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> Submicrospheres by a Self-Template Method</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/large/am0c23069_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c23069&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">To synthesize NaCrO<sub>2</sub>, a mixture of sodium acetate (CH<sub>3</sub>COONa) and the as-synthesized Cr(OH)<sub>3</sub> with a molar ratio of 1:1 was sintered at 900 °C for 5 h under an Ar atmosphere where the Cr(OH)<sub>3</sub> microspheres act as self-templates. Thus, submicrosphere NaCrO<sub>2</sub> was obtained and denoted as s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>. For comparison, we also prepared a NaCrO<sub>2</sub> powder with an irregular morphology by sintering the mixture of CH<sub>3</sub>COONa and a commercial Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> powder under the same conditions, and the product was denoted as i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.2.  Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The structures of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> samples were characterized by employing the strategies that had been described in our previous work. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns for Rietveld refinements were conducted at 2° min<sup>–1</sup>. Thermal gravimetric analysis (TGA, TA, SDT-Q600) was employed in nitrogen between room temperature and 700 °C at 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. The UV absorption spectrum was taken with a UV–vis–NIR spectrophotometer (Shimadzu, SolidSpec-3700). The surface area was analyzed by the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller method (BET) employing an accelerated surface area instrument (ASAP 2020).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical properties of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> samples were tested in coin-type cells (CR2032). The assembling process was the same as our last work. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> The mass loading of the active material was 1.0–2.0 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. For the full cell, Sb/C was employed as the anode, which was prepared according to our previous work <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 34 and 35">(34,35)</a> with a mass loading of around 1.0 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. Before assembling the full cell, the anode was presodiated in a half-cell at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for several cycles to form a stable SEI film and reduce the irreversible capacity in full cells, with the presodiated process end at the half-discharge state. The electrochemical measurement conditions were also similar to our last work <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> (Neware BTS-610, CHI660C). Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests were employed in the initial cycles at 0.1 C. In this measurement, the galvanostatic pulse time was set to 5 min and the subsequent relaxation time was set to 10 min.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">3.1.  Structures of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub></h3><div class="NLM_p">To obtain the NaCrO<sub>2</sub> submicrospheres, the precursor Cr(OH)<sub>3</sub> was first prepared by a hydrothermal process. The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images of Cr(OH)<sub>3</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>a,b, respectively, and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b) show the spherical morphology with a particle size of the secondary particles less than 1 μm. The primary particles of Cr(OH)<sub>3</sub> show the morphology of nanowhiskers, which lead to the hairy edges of the spheres (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>c). The XRD pattern of Cr(OH)<sub>3</sub> in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>d suggests its amorphous phase, which is also confirmed by the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c). After being heated to 700 °C in N<sub>2</sub>, Cr(OH)<sub>3</sub> decomposed into Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>e). There are two strong absorption peaks located at 450 and 600 nm in the UV absorption spectrum of Cr(OH)<sub>3</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>f), which is consistent with the result in the previous report. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> The crystal structures of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> were analyzed by XRD, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. All of the diffraction peaks can be well indexed to the hexagonal O3-phase NaCrO<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS card no. 25-0819) without impurity. Also, the Rietveld refinements of the XRD patterns of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>, suggesting that the morphology has no effect on the crystal phase. The morphology of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> was characterized by applying SEM and TEM (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d,e and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>a–c). Although sintered at 900 °C, the product s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> maintains the spherical morphology of Cr(OH)<sub>3</sub> with submicrospheres smaller than 1 μm. However, the primary particles are nanoflakes with a width less than 50 nm, which could effectively shorten the ion migration path when cycled in SIBs. Meanwhile, i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> shows irregular morphology with primary particle size more than 200 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>a,b). Well-defined lattice fringes with a <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.531 nm can be observed in the HRTEM images of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>c, respectively), corresponding to the (003) planes of NaCrO<sub>2</sub>. The energy-dispersive spectrometry (EDS) mapping (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>g) further reveals the uniform distribution of Na, Cr, and O in s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>. In addition, according to the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>), the specific surface areas of the two samples can be obtained, which are 3.90 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> for s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and 1.24 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> for i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>. On the other hand, assuming s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> microspheres are all dense, its specific surface area would be estimated around 1.5 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> based on the equation: BET = 6/(ρ<i>D</i>), where ρ is the density of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and <i>D</i> is the diameter of the spheres. Therefore, the submicrospheres in s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> must be porous, which is helpful for the electrode kinetics. Nevertheless, the BET of i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> (i.e., 1.24 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) is close to its estimated value, meaning that its particles are rather dense, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/medium/am0c23069_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/large/am0c23069_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. XRD patterns of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> (a). TEM (b) and HRTEM (c) images of Cr(OH)<sub>3</sub>. TEM (d, e) and HRTEM (f) images of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>. EDS mapping images (g) of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/large/am0c23069_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c23069&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10">3.2.  Sodium Storage Behavior of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub></h3><div class="NLM_p">The typical cyclic voltammetry curves of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> tested at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> between 2.0 and 3.6 V are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>a,b, respectively. Two main pairs of redox peaks can be observed on cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> located at 3.04/2.85 and 3.33/3.30 V, with other smaller peaks corresponding to the order/disorder phase transition. Also, the redox voltage positions of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> are almost overlapped in the subsequent cycles, indicating the great reversibility. When comparing the third cycle CV curves as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a, the separation of the redox peaks of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> is smaller than that of i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>, suggesting that the nanoflake-assembled hierarchical structure leads to faster dynamics and lower ion migration resistance in s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>. In addition, the electrochemical performances were tested in half-cells between 2.0 and 3.6 V. The rate properties of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b. The s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> sample exhibits capacity values of 118 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 C and 90 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> even at a quite high current density of 50 C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c). Meanwhile, the i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> sample delivers 86 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 20 C and only 48 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 50 C. It demonstrates that the morphology of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> leads to faster Na<sup>+</sup> migration during the electrochemical process. The submicrosphere NaCrO<sub>2</sub> also shows superior cycling properties. When cycled at 1 C for 300 cycles, s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> maintains 106 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a capacity retention of 91%, while i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> only has 90 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d). When cycled at an elevated current density of 10 C, s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> delivers 98 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 800 cycles with a capacity retention of 90%, which is much higher than 61% of i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e). There is almost no polarization increase on charge/discharge profiles during cycling at 10 C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f) due to the stable structure. More surprisingly, s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> exhibits excellent electrochemical stability at a quite high current density of 20 C, where 90 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is maintained after 1500 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>g,h). The high capacity retention of 87% is obtained after ultralong cycling because of the special stable structure. However, the i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> sample shows worse cycling performance (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). The discharge capacity of i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> decreases rapidly at 20 C, which is only 16 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the 500th cycle, indicating its poor reversibility of the structure. We also tested the electrochemical performances of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> when expanding the voltage to 3.8 and 4.0 V (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>a,b, respectively). The initial discharge capacities obtained are less than 110 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, and the cycle stabilities are poor, which are consistent with the reported result. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> When more Na<sup>+</sup> ions are removed, Cr<sup>4+</sup> will migrate from CrO<sub>6</sub> slabs to Na<sup>+</sup> layers irreversibly, leading to the capacity fading of the NaCrO<sub>2</sub> electrode.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/medium/am0c23069_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/large/am0c23069_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Cyclic voltammetry curves of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (a). Rate behavior of the samples (b). Charge/discharge profiles (c) of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>. Cycling performances of the samples when cycled at 1 C (d) and 10 C (e). Charge/discharge voltage profiles of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> at 10 C (f). Long cycle stability of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> at 20 C (g). Corresponding capacity–voltage relationships in different cycles (h). (1 C = 125 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.)</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/large/am0c23069_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c23069&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To investigate the effect of the nanoflake-assembled submicrosphere hierarchical structure on the ion migration kinetics, the GITT analysis was carried out during the initial sodium ion deintercalation/intercalation process. The GITT curves of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and the corresponding apparent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>) in each current pulse-relaxation section are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a together with the locally enlarged version in the embedded image. The GITT results of i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>. The apparent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients were calculated by the following equation <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 36 to 38">(36−38)</a> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>)<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/medium/am0c23069_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i11"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/medium/am0c23069_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/large/am0c23069_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. GITT profile and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> (a) and partial enlarged version of the GITT profile (inset). Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient comparisons of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> at around 3 V during the charging (b) and discharging (c) process.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/large/am0c23069_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c23069&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>, τ, <i>n</i><sub>m</sub>, <i>V</i><sub>m</sub>, and <i>L</i> are the duration of current pulse, the number of moles, the molar volume of the active materials, and the thickness of the loading slurry, respectively. Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> represents the difference between the equilibrium voltages before and after the pulse. Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> is the variation of cell voltage during a titration step. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b,c reveals the difference between the calculated <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> values of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> at around a 3.0 V plateau in the initial charge and discharge process. Benefiting from the hierarchical submicrosphere structure, s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> shows a faster kinetic process at the 3.0 V plateau. The <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> value of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> varies from 10<sup>–12</sup> to 10<sup>–9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> during the first cycle, while that of i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> is between 10<sup>–14</sup> and 10<sup>–9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>. The calculated average <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> value of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> (5.86 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>) is also higher than that of i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> (3.57 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>). Note that the average <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> calculated here is actually the apparent diffusion coefficient, which absorbs the contribution of porosity. The difference in <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> between s- and i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> is also consistent with their difference in the specific surface area or porosity. Consequently, the facile Na<sup>+</sup> transfer facilitates the sodium storage, hence achieving excellent rate performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was employed to further compare the impedance of the two samples in the half-cells after being activated at 0.2 C for three cycles. The Nyquist plots along with the fitting curves are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>a, and the corresponding linear fitting results of <i>Z</i>′ against ω<sup>–1/2</sup> at the low frequency are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>b. The intercepts of the semicircles with a <i>Z</i>′ axis at high frequency are attributed to the resistances of the electrolyte (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>), and the semicircles represent the charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>). The fitted <i>R</i><sub>s</sub>, <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>, σ (Warburg factor), and calculated <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> are listed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>. The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> (305.0 Ω) is much larger than that of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> (93.9 Ω), suggesting that the nanoflake morphology effectively shortens the ion migration path, thus reducing the ion transfer impedance. These results further confirm the faster migration of Na<sup>+</sup> in s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>. Also, cyclic voltammetry measurements at varied scan rates were conducted (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>). The calculated <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> results are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>, where s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> shows a higher <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> value of 3.96 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> than that of i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> (2.56 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>). By applying these electrochemical measurements, the faster kinetic behavior benefited from the unique morphology in s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> is confirmed.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to further explore the influence of the repeated Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction process on the structure. The crystal structure and the morphology of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> were characterized after 1500 cycles at 20 C. The ex situ XRD reveals that the high crystallinity of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> is kept even after ultralong cycling (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a). In addition to the acetylene black additive, the submicrospheres can be clearly observed in the TEM image without pulverization (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b), suggesting the high structure strength of submicrospheres, against the stress and strain induced by Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation. Therefore, the electrode damage during the charging/discharging process is effectively prevented. The lattice fringes in the HRTEM image also confirm the well-crystalline grains of cycled s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> with no change in the crystal structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). The ultrastable cycling performance and the good rate ability of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> microspheres are among the best when compared with the literature data (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Tables S4 and S5</a>).</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/medium/am0c23069_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/large/am0c23069_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. XRD pattern (a) and TEM (b) and HRTEM (c) images of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> after 1500 cycles at 20 C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/large/am0c23069_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c23069&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12">3.3.  Temperature Effect and Full Cell Behavior</h3><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical properties of the samples at higher and lower temperatures were also measured to fully investigate the function of the hierarchical spherical structure. Compared with i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>, s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> exhibits better sodium storage ability even at 50 °C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a) and −10 °C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b). When tested with different currents of 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, and 20 C at 50 °C, s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> delivers 110, 106, 101, 88, 80, and 50 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, while i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> gives much poorer rate performance, with almost no capacity at 10 and 20 C. In the low-temperature test, s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> delivers 107, 102, 97, 90, and 60 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 C, respectively, while i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> shows worse properties with no capacity at 5 C. The excellent performances of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> further confirm the faster ion migration in s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>, which benefit from the advantages of the hierarchical morphology structure. Hence, it is revealed that the shortened ion diffusion path and the robust structure still favor the electrochemical properties in a wide temperature window.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/medium/am0c23069_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/large/am0c23069_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Rate performances of the samples at 50 °C (a) and −10 °C (b). Illustration of the full cell (c) and its capacity–voltage profiles during the initial cycle at 0.2 C (d). The rate ability (e) and corresponding charge/discharge curves (f) at different rates are also given. The cycling performance of the full cell when cycled at 10 C (g) together with the average variation during cycling (h) are presented.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/large/am0c23069_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c23069&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">In order to explore the prospect for the practical applications, the full cell with Sb/C as the anode material was assembled, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d presents the charging/discharging process of the full cell together with the profiles corresponding to the cathode and anode half-cells. In this work, the N/P ratio of the full cell is around 3. Also, the energy density calculated at 0.2 C based on the cathode mass is 269 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>. The full cell exhibits 111 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 C during the charging/discharging process. When galvanostatically cycled at increased current density (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e,f), the full cell shows 109, 107, 105, 102, 100, 97, and 85 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, 20, and 50 C, respectively. Also, a capacity of 107 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> can be maintained after being recovered to 1 C with no capacity loss, revealing the high reversibility of the unique structure. When tested the full cell at 10 C, a capacity of 100 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is obtained after 300 cycles with a capacity retention of 88% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>g). In addition, the average voltage of the full cell drops slightly from the initial 2.2 to 2.0 V after 300 cycles due to the increasing polarization of the cathode and anode materials during the redox processes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>h). The excellent performances of the full cell demonstrate the promising use of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> in practical applications.</div></div><div id="sec3_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13">3.4.  Sodium Storage Mechanism of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub></h3><div class="NLM_p">The ex situ XRD technique was conducted to clarify the phase transition in the initial sodium ion insertion/desertion process between 2.0 and 3.6 V. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c shows the ex situ XRD patterns at different Na<sub>1–<i>x</i></sub>CrO<sub>2</sub> states corresponding to the typical potential profiles in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d. The enlarged (003) and (006) peaks are listed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a,b, respectively. It can be observed that the (003) peak and (006) peak located at 16.7 and 33.6° shift to a lower angle accompanied with the (110) peak located at 62.4° that shifts to a higher angle during charging, indicating the expansion of the interlayer spacing in the <i>c</i> axis and the contraction of the <i>a</i>–<i>b</i> plane. In addition, the (104) peak becomes broader due to the fact that a new peak appears at the right side of the origin (104) peak, which is attributed to the newly formed monoclinic O3′ phase. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> Also, the monoclinic O3′ phase dominates the structure when charged to 3.1 V. During the subsequent charging process, the peak intensity of the (104) peak decreases and the (015) peak at 45.2° becomes stronger, suggesting the existence of a monoclinic P3 phase. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> The monoclinic O3′ phase gradually disappears, while the P3 phase remains until the end of charging. Therefore, the phase transformation during the charging process can be summarized as follows: hexagonal O3 → monoclinic O3′ → monoclinic P3. When further investigating the discharging process, an inverse process in the structure change can be found, confirming the reversibility of the phase transformation during the electrochemical process. The phase transition behavior is also consistent with the literature studies. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23 ref39" aria-label="reference 22, 23 and 39">(22,23,39)</a></div><figure id="fig6" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/medium/am0c23069_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/large/am0c23069_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Ex situ XRD patterns collected during the initial cycle where s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> was cycled at 0.1 C between 2.0 and 3.6 V. Locally enlarged patterns of (003) (a) and (006) (b) peaks. Ex situ XRD patterns between 10 and 70° (c). Corresponding plots on the initial voltage profiles (d).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/asset/images/large/am0c23069_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c23069&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we designed and synthesized the layered O3-type NaCrO<sub>2</sub> submicrospheres (smaller than 1 μm) through a two-step self-template method as a cathode material for SIBs. The primary particles of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> are nanoflakes with a width less than 50 nm, which effectively shorten the ion diffusion distance. The self-assembled submicrospheres exhibit strong structure robustness with good crystallinity and can retain the spherical structure after ultralong cycle. Therefore, the as-prepared s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> shows good rate capability with a capacity of 90 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> obtained at 50 C owing to the increased apparent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients. Especially, excellent cycle performance is achieved due to the high crystallinity and the stable hierarchical structure. After being tested at 20 C for 1500 cycles, s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> maintains a capacity of 90 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a retention of 87%. In addition, the O3-NaCrO<sub>2</sub> submicrospheres also show advantages at other operating temperatures of 50 and −10 °C. The full cells assembled employing Sb/C as the anode exhibit impressive rate capability with 85 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 50 C and excellent cycling stability. Our successful exploration on the design and synthesis of the hierarchical structure of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> submicrospheres provides a promising strategy to design advanced SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i15">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c23069?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c23069</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM, TEM, XRD, TGA, and UV of Cr(OH)<sub>3</sub>; voltage profiles of s-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>; GITT profile of i-NaCrO<sub>2</sub>; Nyquist plots and EIS fitted results; CV curves at various scan rates and calculation; and comparison with literature data (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c23069/suppl_file/am0c23069_si_001.pdf">am0c23069_si_001.pdf (0.99 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.0c23069" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201900233"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Niu, Y.                 10.1016/j.jcis.2019.06.056</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D553%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D524-529%26journal%3DJ.%2BColloid%2BInterface%2BSci.%26author%3DB.%2BShen%26author%3DR.%2BZhan%26author%3DC.%2BDai%26author%3DY.%2BLi%26author%3DL.%2BHu%26author%3DY.%2BNiu%26author%3DJ.%2BJiang%26author%3DQ.%2BWang%26author%3DM.%2BXu%26title%3DManipulating%2BIrreversible%2BPhase%2BTransition%2Bof%2BNaCrO2%2Btowards%2Ban%2BEffective%2BSodium%2BCompensation%2BAdditive%2Bfor%2BSuperior%2BSodium-Ion%2BFull%2BCells%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.jcis.2019.06.056&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.0c23069&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.jcis.2019.06.056&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Manipulating irreversible phase transition of NaCrO2 towards an effective sodium compensation additive for superior sodium-ion full cells</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Shen, Bolei; Zhan, Renming; Dai, Chunlong; Li, Yi; Hu, Linyu; Niu, Yubin; Jiang, Jian; Wang, Qiang; Xu, Maowen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Colloid and Interface Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">553</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">524-529</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JCISA5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0021-9797</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">During the first charge process of full cells, a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film is formed when the active ion from the cathode is consumed, resulting in irreversible capacity loss.  This phenomenon has shown to be more serious in sodium-ion full cells than in lithium-ion full cells.  Although many strategies have been employed to alleviate the loss of sodium ions, such as presodiation and construction of an artificial solid electrolyte interface, they are both cumbersome and time-consuming.  For the first time, NaCrO2 was used as an effective self-sacrificing sodium compensation additive in sodium-ion full cells due to the irreversible phase transition of NaCrO2 in a high voltage region can deliver an irreversible capacity of up to 230 mAh g-1.  Based on this design, sodium-ion full cells coupled with hard carbon as the anode exhibited higher capacity, less polarization, greater energy d., and superior cycle stability than those of a pristine electrode.  This is mainly attributed to the removal of sodium ions from NaCrO2, which compensates for the loss of sodium ions consumed during the formation of the SEI film on the anode surface during the first charge process.  Overall, this work opens up a new avenue for exploring sodium compensation strategy and contributing to practical application of sodium-ion full cells.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.0c20870"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Niu, Y.-B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, S.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, Y.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Insights on Electrochemical Behaviors of Sodium Peroxide as a Sacrificial Cathode Additive for Boosting Energy Density of Na-Ion Battery</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Insights on Electrochemical Behaviors of Sodium Peroxide as a Sacrificial Cathode Additive for Boosting Energy Density of Na-Ion Battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Guo, Yu-Jie; Niu, Yu-Bin; Wei, Zheng; Zhang, Si-Yuan; Meng, Qinghai; Li, Hongliang; Yin, Ya-Xia; Guo, Yu-Guo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2772-2778</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The development of Na-ion full cells (NIFCs) suffers from the issue that the solid electrolyte interphase formation on the carbon anode consumes the limited sodium from cathode and thus incurs the decreased energy d. and poor cyclic stability.  To address these issues, we herein report that Na2O2 could be used as a sacrificial Na source through spraying its slurry on the surface of cathode, and investigate its stability as well as electrochem. behavior toward NIFCs.  The results show that Na2O2 has good chem. and storage stability under a dry atm. and has no neg. effect on the electrochem. performance of the cathode.  Compared with the pristine cathode, the Na2O2-decorated cathode exhibits higher discharge capacity, superior capacity retention, and rate capability in a full cell with a carbon anode.  Our cathode Na compensation strategy provides an effective avenue to make up for the irreversible Na+ loss cause by the formation of solid electrolyte interphase on the anode, thereby promoting the electrochem. performance and energy d. of NIFCs toward the large-scale application.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1126/sciadv.aav7412"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Approaching High-Performance Potassium-Ion Batteries via Advanced Design Strategies and Engineering</span>. <i>Sci.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enabling Superior Electrochemical Properties for Highly Efficient Potassium Storage by Impregnating Ultrafine Sb Nanocrystals within Nanochannel-Containing Carbon Nanofibers</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ge, Xufang; Liu, Shuhu; Qiao, Man; Du, Yichen; Li, Yafei; Bao, Jianchun; Zhou, Xiaosi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sb-based nanocomposites are attractive anode materials for batteries as they exhibit large theor. capacity and impressive working voltage.  However, tardy potassium ion diffusion characteristics, unstable Sb/electrolyte interphase, and huge vol. variation pose a challenge, hindering their practical use for potassium-ion batteries (PIBs).  Now, a simple robust strategy is presented for uniformly impregnating ultrasmall Sb nanocrystals within carbon nanofibers contg. an array of hollow nanochannels (denoted u-Sb@CNFs), resolving the issues above and yielding high-performance PIBs. u-Sb@CNFs can be directly employed as an anode, thereby dispensing with the need for conductive additives and binders.  Such a judiciously crafted u-Sb@CNF-based anode renders a set of intriguing electrochem. properties, representing large charge capacity, unprecedented cycling stability, and outstanding rate performance.  A reversible capacity of 225 mAh g-1 is retained after 2000 cycles at 1 A g-1.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1016/j.jechem.2020.10.039"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yi, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lai, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Coupling Co<sub>3</sub>[Co(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> Nanocubes with Reduced Graphene Oxide for High-Rate and Long-Cycle-Life Potassium Storage</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Coupling Co3[Co(CN)6]2 nanocubes with reduced graphene oxide for high-rate and long-cycle-life potassium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Yifan; Du, Yichen; Yi, Zuyue; Zhang, Zhuangzhuang; Lai, Chenling; Liao, Jiaying; Zhou, Xiaosi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Energy Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">58</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">593-601</span>CODEN:

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2095-4956</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Science Press</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">As one of prussian blue analogs, Co3[Co(CN)6]2 has been explored as a promising anode material for potassium-ion batteries (PIBs) owing to its high potassium storage capacity.  Unfortunately, Co3[Co(CN)6]2 possesses low electronic cond. and its structure collapses easily during potassiation and depotassiation, resulting in poor rate performance and cyclic stability.  To solve these problems, we develop a facile multi-step method to successfully combine uniform Co3[Co(CN)6]2 nanocubes with rGO by C-O-Co bonds.  As expected, these chemcial bonds shorten the distance between Co3[Co(CN)6]2 and rGO to the angstrom meter level, which significantly improve the electronic cond. of Co3[Co(CN)6]2.  Besides, the complete encapsulation of Co3[Co(CN)6]2 nanocubes by rGO endows the structure of Co3[Co(CN)6]2 with high stability, thus withstanding repeated insertion/extn. of potassium-ions without visible morphol. and structural changes.  Benefiting from the above-mentioned structural advantages, the Co3[Co(CN)6]2/rGO nanocomposite exhibits a high reversible capacity of 400.8 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 0.1 A g-1, an exceptional rate capability of 115.5 mAh g-1 at 5 A g-1, and an ultralong cycle life of 231.9 mAh g-1 at 0.1 A g-1 after 1000 cycles.  Addnl., the effects of different amts. of rGO and different sizes of Co3[Co(CN)6]2 nanocubes on the potassium storage performance are also studied.  This work offers an ideal route to significantly enhance the electrochem. properties of prussian blue analogs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref10" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201200691"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Slater, M.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1039/C6CS00776G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Myung, S.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.-K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium-Ion Batteries: Present and Future</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1002/anie.202008318"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Q.-C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Y.-N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Yolk-Shell-Structured FePO<sub>4</sub> Cathode for High-Rate and Long-Cycling Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Angew.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Amorphous iron phosphate (FePO4) has attracted enormous attention as a promising cathode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) because of its high theor. specific capacity and superior electrochem. reversibility.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Isostructural and Multivalent Anion Substitution toward Improved Phosphate Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Mei-Yi; Guo, Jin-Zhi; Wang, Zhi-Wei; Gu, Zhen-Yi; Nie, Xue-Jiao; Yang, Xu; Wu, Xing-Long</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Polyanion-type phosphate materials are highly promising cathode candidates for next-generation batteries due to their excellent structural stability during cycling; however, their poor cond. has impeded their development.  Isostructural and multivalent anion substitution combined with carbon coating is proposed to greatly improve the electrochem. properties of phosphate cathode in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  Specifically, multivalent tetrahedral SiO44- substitute for PO43- in Na3V2(PO4)3 (NVP) lattice, prepg. the optimal Na3.1V2(PO4)2.9(SiO4)0.1 with high-rate capability (delivering a high capacity of 82.5 mAh g-1 even at 20 C) and outstanding cyclic stability (≈98% capacity retention after 500 cycles at 1 C).  Theor. calcn. and exptl. analyses reveal that the anion-substituted Na3.1V2(PO4)2.9(SiO4)0.1 reduces the bandgap of NVP lattice and enhanced its structural stability, Na+-diffusion kinetics and electronic cond.  This strategy of multivalent and isostructural anion substitution chem. provides a new insight to develop advanced phosphate cathodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1039/C9TA04274A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Niu, Y.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A review on pyrophosphate framework cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Niu, Yubin; Zhang, Yan; Xu, Maowen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
